2x" width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Hurricane Elena</h1>\n<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\n<div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">\n\t\t\t\t\tJump to:\t\t\t\t\t<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, \t\t\t\t\t<a total</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row" style="padding:0px 2px;white-space:nowrap">Damage</th>\n<td style="padding:0px 2px">$1.3 billion (1985 <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">USD</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row" style="padding:0px 2px;white-space:nowrap">Areas affected</th>\n<td style="padding:0px 2px"><a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (<a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;padding:0px 2px"></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center; border-top:#aaa 1px solid;">Part of the <b><a href="/wiki/1985_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1985 Atlantic hurricane season">1985 Atlantic hurricane season</a></b></td>\n</tr>\n<tr style="display:none">\n<td colspan="2"></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p><b>Hurricane Elena</b> was an unpredictable and damaging <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclone</a> that affected eastern and central portions of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="Gulf Coast of the United States">United States Gulf Coast</a> in late August and early September 1985. Threatening popular tourist destinations during <a href="/wiki/Labor_Day" title="Labor Day">Labor Day weekend</a>, Elena repeatedly deviated from its forecast path, triggering evacuations of unprecedented extent. The hurricane wrought havoc to property and the environment between southwestern <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, though lesser effects were felt well beyond those areas. Elena developed on August 28 near <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, and after traveling lengthwise across the island with little impact, it entered the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> and continued to strengthen. Initially projected to strike the central Gulf Coast, the hurricane unexpectedly veered toward the east on August 30, then stalled just 50\xc2\xa0mi (80\xc2\xa0km) west of <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Key,_Florida" title="Cedar Key, Florida">Cedar Key, Florida</a>. Despite predictions that Elena would continue eastward across Florida, the cyclone remained nearly stationary for about 48 hours, causing damage all along the eastern gulf with high winds and waves, before slowly moving northwest and ultimately making <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landfall_(meteorology)" title="Landfall (meteorology)">landfall</a> near <a href="/wiki/Biloxi,_Mississippi" title="Biloxi, Mississippi">Biloxi, Mississippi</a>, on September 2 as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffir-Simpson_Hurricane_Scale#Category_3" title="Saffir-Simpson Hurricane Scale">Category 3</a> major hurricane. The storm quickly weakened upon moving ashore and dissipated on September 4.</p>\n<p>The hurricane\'s unpredictable shifts in direction created what was considered the largest peacetime evacuation in the nation\'s history. Evacuations occurred in sequence to follow the storm\'s forecast positions, and many residents and tourists along portions of the Gulf Coast were forced to leave twice in a matter of days. Preparations were generally timely and efficient, though accommodations and resources at storm shelters were stretched thin, and many refugees tried to return home against officials\' orders. About 1.25 million people fled the storm in Florida alone, contributing to a region-wide total of nearly 2 million evacuees. <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_warnings_and_watches" title="Tropical cyclone warnings and watches">Tropical cyclone warnings and watches</a> were continuously issued and adjusted, and forecasters stressed the storm\'s destructive potential for days.</p>\n<p>Elena\'s slow movement off western Florida resulted in severe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beach_erosion" title="Beach erosion">beach erosion</a> and damage to coastal buildings, roads, and <a href="/wiki/Seawall" title="Seawall">seawalls</a>, especially to those of old or inadequate construction. Destruction was greatest near the shore and on islands such as Cedar Key and <a href="/wiki/Dog_Island_(Florida)" title="Dog Island (Florida)">Dog Island</a>, though tornadoes spawned by the hurricane swept through communities and mobile home parks well inland. The hurricane devastated the <a href="/wiki/Apalachicola_Bay" title="Apalachicola Bay">Apalachicola Bay</a> shellfish industry, killing large quantities of oysters, destroying their reefs, and leaving thousands of workers unemployed. Farther west, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dauphin_Island" title="Dauphin Island">Dauphin Island</a> in Alabama endured wind gusts as high as 130\xc2\xa0mph (210\xc2\xa0km/h) and a significant storm surge. The island sustained some of the most significant damage inflicted by Elena, including several hundred damaged or demolished homes. The rest of the state\'s coast also sustained considerable damage, and the inland pecan and soybean crops were severely diminished in Alabama and Mississippi.</p>\n<p>Over 13,000 homes were damaged in Mississippi, and 200 were destroyed. Cities close to the Alabama border\xe2\x80\x94including <a href="/wiki/Pascagoula,_Mississippi" title="Pascagoula, Mississippi">Pascagoula</a>\xe2\x80\x94experienced widespread damage to residences, schools, and businesses, and the community of <a href="/wiki/Gautier,_Mississippi" title="Gautier, Mississippi">Gautier</a> was effectively isolated from the outside world. Several apparent but unconfirmed tornadoes appear to have exacerbated the damage in the <a href="/wiki/Gulfport,_Mississippi" title="Gulfport, Mississippi">Gulfport</a> area. Wind damage extended into portions of eastern Louisiana. Overall, nine people died as a result of the hurricane: two in <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> due to drownings in rip currents, three in Florida, two in Louisiana, one in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, and one in a maritime accident in the Gulf of Mexico. Damage totaled about $1.3 billion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and power outages from the storm affected 550,000 people. In Elena\'s wake, President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> declared parts of Alabama, Mississippi, and Florida federal disaster areas, making storm victims eligible for financial aid and temporary housing. The name <i>Elena</i> was later retired from the cyclical list of Atlantic hurricane names because of the storm\'s effects.</p>\n<p></p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc">\n<div links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteorological_history">Meteorological history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Meteorological history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elena_1985_track.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1933" data-file-width="3000" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Elena_1985_track.png/220px-Elena_1985_track.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Elena_1985_track.png/330px-Elena_1985_track.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Elena_1985_track.png/440px-Elena_1985_track.png 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elena_1985_track.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMap plotting the track and intensity of the storm, according to the <a href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_scale" title="Saffir\xe2\x80\x93Simpson scale">Saffir\xe2\x80\x93Simpson scale</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The origins of Hurricane Elena trace to an easterly <a href="/wiki/Tropical_wave" title="Tropical wave">tropical wave</a> that was first identified off the western coast of Africa on August 23, 1985. The system sped westward across the Atlantic at up to 35\xc2\xa0mph (56\xc2\xa0km/h). Its rapid motion, combined with the presence of an unusually hostile <a href="/wiki/Saharan_Air_Layer" title="Saharan Air Layer">Saharan Air Layer</a>, prevented <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclogenesis" title="Tropical cyclogenesis">tropical cyclogenesis</a> for several days. Driven by a strong subtropical <a href="/wiki/Ridge_(meteorology)" title="Ridge (meteorology)">ridge</a> to its north, the wave quickly approached North America as it began to show signs of organization. At 00:00 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Coordinated_Time" title="Universal Coordinated Time">UTC</a> on August 28, the disturbance developed into a tropical depression while over the <a href="/wiki/Windward_Passage" title="Windward Passage">Windward Passage</a>. The newly designated depression began to track west-northwestward over <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, which is known to disrupt tropical cyclone development. Despite that, its central <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barometric_pressure" title="Barometric pressure">barometric pressure</a> continued to deepen, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane_Hunters" title="Hurricane Hunters">reconnaissance aircraft</a> found winds exceeding 50\xc2\xa0mph (80\xc2\xa0km/h) near the center. In response, the <a href="/wiki/National_Hurricane_Center" title="National Hurricane Center">National Hurricane Center</a> upgraded the depression to Tropical Storm Elena over northern Cuba later on August 28.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-prelim1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hurdat_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-hurdat-3">[3]</a></sup></p>\n<p>After passing north of <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a>, Cuba, Elena emerged into the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>. At 12:00 UTC on August 29, Elena intensified into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_Hurricane_Scale#Category_1" title="Saffir\xe2\x80\x93Simpson Hurricane Scale">Category 1 hurricane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hurdat_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-hurdat-3">[3]</a></sup> Analysis of steering currents through the morning of August 30 suggested that Elena would continue on its northwestward track, striking the area between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Orleans,_Louisiana" title="New Orleans, Louisiana">New Orleans, Louisiana</a>, and Biloxi, Mississippi within 30 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">[4]</a></sup> Unexpectedly, a mid-to-upper-level <a href="/wiki/Trough_(meteorology)" title="Trough (meteorology)">trough</a> of low pressure diving in from the northwest created a weakness in the easterly currents, allowing Elena to recurve and slow drastically in forward speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-prelim1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpc_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-hpc-5">[5]</a></sup> Roughly 24 hours after attaining hurricane intensity, the storm abruptly turned east in response to the trough.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim1_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-prelim1-2">[2]</a></sup> Having defied initial forecasts, Hurricane Elena drifted on its new course toward the coast of northwest <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>. Forecasters now called for the trough to direct the hurricane across the Florida Peninsula and into the western Atlantic. However, the relatively weak trough moved rapidly, and instead of fully engaging Elena, its axis passed over the storm\'s center. Further, post-storm analysis of <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> imagery suggested that the hurricane split the trough into two distinct segments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">[4]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_August_30_500_mb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="635" data-file-width="837" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Hurricane_Elena_August_30_500_mb.jpg/220px-Hurricane_Elena_August_30_500_mb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Hurricane_Elena_August_30_500_mb.jpg/330px-Hurricane_Elena_August_30_500_mb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Hurricane_Elena_August_30_500_mb.jpg/440px-Hurricane_Elena_August_30_500_mb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_August_30_500_mb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Geopotential_height" title="Geopotential height">Geopotential height</a> chart for the 500-millibar level, showing Elena (the closed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isohypse#Barometric_pressure" title="Isohypse">isohypse</a> over the Gulf of Mexico) on August 30. The trough that turned Elena toward the east can be seen embedded within the flow over the United States, and the ridge responsible for eventually pushing the storm back is denoted to the east of Florida.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Extrapolation from the storm\'s eastward progress yielded a projected landfall location near <a href="/wiki/Levy_County,_Florida" title="Levy County, Florida">Levy County</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes252_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes252-6">[6]</a></sup> However, after the passage of the upper-level system early on August 31, steering currents slackened, and Elena became nearly stationary in the extreme northeastern Gulf of Mexico. At its closest, the center of the storm was around 50\xc2\xa0mi (80\xc2\xa0km) from <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Key,_Florida" title="Cedar Key, Florida">Cedar Key, Florida</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim1_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-prelim1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpc_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-hpc-5">[5]</a></sup> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maximum_sustained_winds" title="Maximum sustained winds">maximum sustained winds</a> estimated at 105\xc2\xa0mph (170\xc2\xa0km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim2_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-prelim2-7">[7]</a></sup> Elena\'s intensity remained consistent, and the cyclone was able to continue strengthening as soon as movement resumed. Early on September 1, Elena reached Category 3 major hurricane status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hurdat_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-hurdat-3">[3]</a></sup> An area of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_pressure_system" title="High pressure system">high pressure</a> soon began to build over the eastern United States, causing Elena to slowly retrograde westward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim2_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-prelim2-7">[7]</a></sup> For much of September 1, the center of the hurricane was within range of the <a href="/wiki/WSR-57" title="WSR-57">WSR-57</a> radar station in <a href="/wiki/Apalachicola,_Florida" title="Apalachicola, Florida">Apalachicola, Florida</a>, enabling extensive study of small features within the <a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)" title="Eye (cyclone)">eye</a> and surrounding eyewall. During that period of observation, the previously unobstructed eye became cloud-filled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The hurricane accelerated on a trajectory toward the central <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Gulf_Coast" title="U.S. Gulf Coast">U.S. Gulf Coast</a>, sliding south of the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Panhandle" title="Florida Panhandle">Florida Panhandle</a>. During the afternoon of September 1, the hurricane attained its peak intensity, with winds of 125\xc2\xa0mph (205\xc2\xa0km/h) as confirmed by reconnaissance aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim2_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-prelim2-7">[7]</a></sup> On the morning of September 2, Elena approached coastal Mississippi from the east-southeast, still at major hurricane status. It <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landfall_(meteorology)" title="Landfall (meteorology)">came ashore</a> close to Biloxi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim2_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-prelim2-7">[7]</a></sup> which was coincidentally within the hurricane\'s first forecast destination range before its extended detour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">[4]</a></sup> Once inland, the hurricane immediately deteriorated, weakening to a tropical storm just hours after landfall,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hurdat_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-hurdat-3">[3]</a></sup> and its center rapidly <a href="/wiki/Ageostrophy" title="Ageostrophy">filled</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks17_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks17-9">[9]</a></sup> The system curved northwestward over <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, and despite weakening, it continued to ignite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convection_(meteorology)" title="Convection (meteorology)">thunderstorm activity</a> which spawned heavy rains. Elena persisted for several days before degenerating into a remnant area of low pressure on September 4. Its associated cloud structure became distorted on September 5, and dissipated over <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> that same night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpc_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-hpc-5">[5]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparations">Preparations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Preparations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The unpredictable nature of the hurricane, in conjunction with its arrival at popular tourist destinations on the <a href="/wiki/Labor_Day" title="Labor Day">Labor Day</a> holiday weekend, severely complicated preparations along the Gulf Coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes252_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes252-6">[6]</a></sup> Evacuations and the hoisting of weather advisories inadvertently occurred in stages to keep up with Elena\'s shifts in direction; <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_warnings_and_watches#Hurricanes" title="Tropical cyclone warnings and watches">hurricane warnings</a> were in effect at one point or another for every coastal location between <a href="/wiki/Morgan_City,_Louisiana" title="Morgan City, Louisiana">Morgan City, Louisiana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sarasota,_Florida" title="Sarasota, Florida">Sarasota, Florida</a>. Much of the northern Gulf Coast was under a hurricane warning on two separate occasions for two different trajectories of the storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim4_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-prelim4-10">[10]</a></sup> Evacuations of residents and vacationers also overlapped in many cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes252_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes252-6">[6]</a></sup> Collectively, this led to the "largest number of people ever evacuated", according to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Case" title="Robert Case">Robert Case</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup> Some evacuees moved inland to meet relatives, but many stayed relatively local, filling hotels and designated shelters such as schools and churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes252_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes252-6">[6]</a></sup> Despite the unusually fluid scenario, officials were well aware of the storm\'s destructive power days before its actual landfall. National Hurricane Center hurricane expert <a href="/wiki/Bob_Sheets" title="Bob Sheets">Bob Sheets</a> cautioned on August 30 that Elena "will be over a $1 billion storm".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-preps1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-preps1-12">[12]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elena_01_sep_1985_1253Z_N8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1340" data-file-width="1240" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Elena_01_sep_1985_1253Z_N8.jpg/300px-Elena_01_sep_1985_1253Z_N8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Elena_01_sep_1985_1253Z_N8.jpg/450px-Elena_01_sep_1985_1253Z_N8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Elena_01_sep_1985_1253Z_N8.jpg/600px-Elena_01_sep_1985_1253Z_N8.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elena_01_sep_1985_1253Z_N8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nHurricane Elena early on September 1, shortly before attaining peak intensity and accelerating to the west</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>During Elena\'s initial approach, the first series of hurricane warnings were issued between <a href="/wiki/Grand_Isle,_Louisiana" title="Grand Isle, Louisiana">Grand Isle, Louisiana</a>, and Apalachicola, Florida. The storm\'s projected path quickly nudged westward, prompting the warnings to be extended to Morgan City, Louisiana, and truncated to <a href="/wiki/Pensacola,_Florida" title="Pensacola, Florida">Pensacola, Florida</a> on their eastern reach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks29_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks29-13">[13]</a></sup> Heeding the advisories, nearly one million residents and vacationers fled the storm\'s path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes252_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes252-6">[6]</a></sup> Personnel on offshore oil rigs in the northern Gulf of Mexico began leaving as early as August 29.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The governors of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> declared states of emergency by August 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Huge crowds formed at stores as individuals searched for emergency supplies, and simultaneously, lines grew at gas stations. Due to the impending danger, many businesses closed and fortified their buildings. Across the Gulf Coast, classes at schools were cancelled, and residents in the New Orleans area were particularly wary of what was being called the first serious hurricane threat in 20 years (<a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Betsy" title="Hurricane Betsy">Hurricane Betsy</a> caused catastrophic flooding in and around New Orleans in 1965).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In Mississippi, the mass exodus created bumper-to-bumper traffic on crucial highways, such as the west\xe2\x80\x93east <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_90" title="U.S. Route 90">U.S. Route 90</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In Florida, then-Governor <a href="/wiki/Bob_Graham" title="Bob Graham">Bob Graham</a> activated 250 National Guard troops on August 30 to facilitate efficient evacuations, stating that 1,600 more were on standby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> By that time, it became evident that Elena would head farther east than initially expected, stirring more concern for the eastern Gulf Coast. Accordingly, hurricane warnings were dropped for coastal Louisiana west of Grand Isle and replaced eastward to Apalachicola, Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-preps1_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-preps1-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks30_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks30-19">[19]</a></sup></p>\n<p>By the evening of August 30, after Elena\'s sharp turn to the east, hurricane warnings along most of the northern Gulf Coast were discontinued. In accordance, evacuees between Louisiana and the four westernmost counties of the Florida Panhandle returned home as shelters closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks30_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks30-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulf_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-gulf-20">[20]</a></sup> With the storm\'s new course, the area of highest threat translated east to the remainder of the Panhandle and the western Florida Peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes252_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes252-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulf_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-gulf-20">[20]</a></sup> As such, Governor Graham recommended evacuations south to the <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa</a> area late on August 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks30_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks30-19">[19]</a></sup> A mandatory evacuation was then issued overnight for ten more coastal counties, encompassing 573,000 affected individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes252_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes252-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulf_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-gulf-20">[20]</a></sup> On August 31, Governor Graham advised residents in vulnerable areas of 15 inland counties to find safer ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1million_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-1million-21">[21]</a></sup> In response to the heightening danger, most of the National Guard troops previously on standby were sent to block access to certain areas, and an additional 3,000 were placed on standby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guards_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-guards-22">[22]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In the greater <a href="/wiki/St._Petersburg,_Florida" title="St. Petersburg, Florida">St. Petersburg</a>, Pinellas County, area alone, 320,000 people evacuated ahead of the storm in what was a national record for the largest evacuation of a single county in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redcross_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-redcross-24">[24]</a></sup> The large number of refugees from the storm put a strain on facilities, highways, and contingencies. Although the number of people required to leave far exceeded the capacity of Pinellas County shelters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulf_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-gulf-20">[20]</a></sup> only 120,000 of the 300,000 or more refugees made use of the shelters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redcross_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-redcross-24">[24]</a></sup> Still, official shelter usage was considered to be higher than average, possibly due to shortened lead times limiting the ability of individuals to make arrangements with friends and relatives, or increased awareness of available resources. Post-storm phone surveys indicated that evacuation order compliance rates were as high as 90% in Pinellas County,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and the entire evacuation there took just 9 hours, rather than the expected 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redcross_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-redcross-24">[24]</a></sup> With over 200,000 individuals recorded to be in more than 120 shelters along the coast of west-central Florida, evacuees became restless as a result of the duration of the storm. Supplies such as food ran short, and many people ignored orders and tried to return home prematurely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guards_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-guards-22">[22]</a></sup> The threat of Hurricane Elena also triggered an unprecedented mass transfer of medical and nursing home patients. <a href="/wiki/Tampa_General_Hospital" title="Tampa General Hospital">Tampa General Hospital</a>, at 84% of patient capacity, was evacuated; four more hospitals and around 19 nursing homes in Pinellas County were also cleared. Overall, nearly 2,000 nursing home patients were transported to safety. Although successful, the process encountered issues such as time constraints and staffing shortages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Late on September 1, when the storm began to retrograde, hurricane warnings were reinstated westward along the coast to Grand Isle, Louisiana,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes253_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes253-27">[27]</a></sup> as advisories along the west coast of the Florida Peninsula were allowed to expire. By the time officials lifted evacuation orders, the number of evacuees staying in shelters already decreased significantly due to the rampant eagerness and anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lakeland_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-lakeland-28">[28]</a></sup> Roughly 250,000 people in the Florida Panhandle, 175,000 in Alabama, 70,000 in Mississippi, and 50,000 in Louisiana\xe2\x80\x94a total of 545,000\xe2\x80\x94were ordered to leave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-four_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-four-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pont_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-pont-30">[30]</a></sup> Several hundred thousand of the people affected by the new string of evacuation orders had also been forced to leave just days earlier, and in extreme cases had one day or less reprieve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lakeland_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-lakeland-28">[28]</a></sup> Governor Graham\'s office reported that during the entire storm event, 1.25 million people from Florida evacuated at some point,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-la_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-la-31">[31]</a></sup> and state police in Louisiana estimated that figure in their state to be around 400,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roars_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-roars-32">[32]</a></sup> In total, nearly 2 million people fled the storm over its entire course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Research_Division" title="Hurricane Research Division">Hurricane Research Division</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Oceanographic_and_Meteorological_Laboratory" title="Atlantic Oceanographic and Meteorological Laboratory">Atlantic Oceanographic and Meteorological Laboratory</a>, Hurricane Elena produced Category 3 winds (111\xc2\xa0mph (179\xc2\xa0km/h) or greater) in Florida, Alabama, and Mississippi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> reported that in addition to the hundreds of single-family homes demolished by the hurricane, over 17,000 sustained some degree of damage; thousands of mobile homes, apartments, and condominiums were also damaged or destroyed. A forecaster at the National Hurricane Center determined the worst of the hurricane\'s effects were focused around <a href="/wiki/Dauphin_Island,_Alabama" title="Dauphin Island, Alabama">Dauphin Island, Alabama</a>, and Pascagoula, Mississippi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harlan_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-harlan-35">[35]</a></sup> though noteworthy damage occurred across large areas of Louisiana, Mississippi, Alabama, and Florida, with effects documented as far west as <a href="/wiki/South_Padre_Island,_Texas" title="South Padre Island, Texas">South Padre Island, Texas</a>, and as far north as <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>. Nine deaths were attributed to the hurricane in four states and on the waters of the Gulf of Mexico, and 134 people along Elena\'s path were hospitalized, many of them due to storm-related stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim3_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-prelim3-36">[36]</a></sup> Power outages plagued the entire region, affecting about 550,000 customers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-550re_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-550re-37">[37]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Climatic_Data_Center" title="National Climatic Data Center">National Climatic Data Center</a> compiled a total monetary damage figure of $1.3 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncdc_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncdc-38">[38]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In addition to its effects over land, Elena also affected offshore interests. When a cargo ship close to the hurricane\'s center rolled in high seas on August 29, two unsecured storage containers collided, crushing a man to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes255_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes255-39">[39]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">oil platform</a> operated by <a href="/wiki/ExxonMobil" title="ExxonMobil">Exxon</a> and anchored off the coast of Pensacola, Florida, was ripped from its moorings and reported missing on September 2. The oil rig drifted 14\xc2\xa0mi (23\xc2\xa0km) away before it was spotted by a Coast Guard aircraft. Exxon previously evacuated the platform after rough seas snapped the first two of its eight anchoring cables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-after_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-after-40">[40]</a></sup> Damage was reported on four other offshore platforms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and a 6\xc2\xa0in (150\xc2\xa0mm) oil pipeline broke during the hurricane at an estimated cost of $1.6 million (the same pipeline broke two more times during the 1985 hurricane season).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmsd10_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-mmsd10-42">[42]</a></sup></p>\n<p>A large aspect of the hurricane\'s devastation was the havoc it wrought on the eastern Gulf of Mexico <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oyster</a> industry, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Apalachicola_Bay" title="Apalachicola Bay">Apalachicola Bay</a> area and off the coast of Alabama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oyster_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-oyster-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apa_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-apa-44">[44]</a></sup> Elena subjected the Apalachicola Bay reefs to high winds, strong tidal action, and moderate to heavy rainfall, churning up huge quantities of silt and mud which suffocated up to 90% of live oysters and virtually destroyed the most important harvesting sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oyster_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-oyster-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmsd20_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-mmsd20-45">[45]</a></sup> Commercial harvesting was suspended until May 1986, at which time some of the prominent reefs of the eastern Apalachicola Bay system were deemed able to sustain oyster-catching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Low astronomical tides amplified the effects of severe turbulence in the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apa_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-apa-44">[44]</a></sup> Thousands of individuals relying on the Apalachicola Bay oyster industry soon found themselves struggling to make a living;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oyster_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-oyster-43">[43]</a></sup> losses in production at two major reefs were expected to surpass $30 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmsd20_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-mmsd20-45">[45]</a></sup> All of Alabama\'s major reefs were affected by the hurricane, and its most productive was nearly destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmsd10_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-mmsd10-42">[42]</a></sup> The problem was later confounded in November by Hurricane Kate, which, according to the National Hurricane Center, "[dealt] the final blow" to certain oyster beds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Florida">Florida</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Florida">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_Florida_road_damage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="897" data-file-width="600" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Hurricane_Elena_Florida_road_damage.jpg/220px-Hurricane_Elena_Florida_road_damage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Hurricane_Elena_Florida_road_damage.jpg/330px-Hurricane_Elena_Florida_road_damage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Hurricane_Elena_Florida_road_damage.jpg/440px-Hurricane_Elena_Florida_road_damage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_Florida_road_damage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nNumerous coastal roads, such as this one along the Gulf Coast of Florida, were damaged by the hurricane\'s storm surge.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The storm began affecting Florida late on August 28 and early on August 29. In <a href="/wiki/Key_West,_Florida" title="Key West, Florida">Key West</a>, on the east side of Elena\'s strengthening center, wind gusts exceeded 50\xc2\xa0mph (80\xc2\xa0km/h), accompanied by 1.8\xc2\xa0in (46\xc2\xa0mm) of rain and higher-than-normal tides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup> Several boats washed ashore at <a href="/wiki/Smathers_Beach" title="Smathers Beach">Smathers Beach</a>. Similarly adverse conditions occurred throughout rest of the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Keys" title="Florida Keys">Keys</a> and across the southern Florida Peninsula; 60\xc2\xa0mph (100\xc2\xa0km/h) wind gusts and modest rainfall stretched as far east as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miami,_Florida" title="Miami, Florida">Miami</a> area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Easterly winds produced <a href="/wiki/Significant_wave_height" title="Significant wave height">significant wave heights</a> of 5.2\xc2\xa0ft (1.6\xc2\xa0m) at <a href="/wiki/West_Palm_Beach,_Florida" title="West Palm Beach, Florida">West Palm Beach</a> and 10.5\xc2\xa0ft (3.2\xc2\xa0m) at <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville</a>, along the Atlantic coast of Florida, by August 31.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge6_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge6-49">[49]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Outer <a href="/wiki/Rainband" title="Rainband">rainbands</a> of the large hurricane produced squally weather over parts of northern Florida as early as the morning of August 30. By then, the low-lying coastline near Apalachicola already began to flood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Elena would continue to affect the state for several days as it meandered offshore, resulting in moderate to heavy rainfall. Upwards of 10\xc2\xa0in (250\xc2\xa0mm) accumulated in many locations, peaking at 15.67\xc2\xa0in (398\xc2\xa0mm) near <a href="/wiki/Cross_City,_Florida" title="Cross City, Florida">Cross City</a> and reaching 11.31\xc2\xa0in (287\xc2\xa0mm) at Apalachicola. Farther south in the <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa</a> area, the precipitation was less significant, exceeding 5\xc2\xa0in (130\xc2\xa0mm) at <a href="/wiki/Clearwater,_Florida" title="Clearwater, Florida">Clearwater</a>. Parts of the state\'s northeastern coast\xe2\x80\x94farther away from the hurricane\'s center\xe2\x80\x94also saw formidable rainfall, with a local maximum of 10.57\xc2\xa0in (268\xc2\xa0mm) at Jacksonville.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpc_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-hpc-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup> Still, those totals represented a relatively dry storm, considering its long duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks16_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks16-51">[51]</a></sup> Despite initial hopes that the hurricane would help alleviate drought conditions across interior portions of southern Florida, precipitation there was generally inconsequential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Storm-heightened tides extended along the Florida coast as far south as Sarasota and generally ran a modest 3 to 6\xc2\xa0ft (0.91 to 1.83\xc2\xa0m) above normal, though their duration and extent proved noteworthy. The highest recorded <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surge</a> associated with the hurricane was 10\xc2\xa0ft (3.0\xc2\xa0m) at Apalachicola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim2_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-prelim2-7">[7]</a></sup> The combination of raised water levels and strong waves resulted in severe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beach_erosion" title="Beach erosion">erosion</a> along many beaches. Many homes near the water were destroyed by the surge, and shoreline structures such as docks, causeways, bridges, low-lying roads, and seawalls sustained substantial damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes253_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes253-27">[27]</a></sup> Several large fishing piers were either partially or totally destroyed; notably, the city pier at <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Key,_Florida" title="Cedar Key, Florida">Cedar Key</a> and the popular 1,500\xc2\xa0ft (460\xc2\xa0m) <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rocks_Beach,_Florida" title="Indian Rocks Beach, Florida">Big Indian Rocks</a> Fishing Pier were both demolished by the hurricane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-piers_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-piers-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Debris from the Big Indian Rocks Fishing Pier drifted northward toward <a href="/wiki/Pinellas_County,_Florida#Barrier_islands_and_passes" title="Pinellas County, Florida">Clearwater Pass</a> and accumulated along private beaches at <a href="/wiki/Belleair_Shore,_Florida" title="Belleair Shore, Florida">Belleair Shore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-piers_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-piers-53">[53]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The storm\'s strongest winds remained largely over open waters, although severe gusts still brushed coastal cities and barrier islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge12_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge12-55">[55]</a></sup> The strongest winds were observed in two areas of the coast: from Cedar Key to Clearwater, and from Apalachicola to Pensacola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge12_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge12-55">[55]</a></sup> Official gust reports included 75\xc2\xa0mph (121\xc2\xa0km/h) at Cedar Key and nearly 70\xc2\xa0mph (110\xc2\xa0km/h) at Clearwater; later, on September 2, a gust of 90\xc2\xa0mph (140\xc2\xa0km/h) was observed at Pensacola along the Florida Panhandle, with sustained winds exceeding 50\xc2\xa0mph (80\xc2\xa0km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim2_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-prelim2-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup> Winds in Franklin County approached 125\xc2\xa0mph (201\xc2\xa0km/h) by unofficial estimates. The storm\'s effects were not limited to the shore, however, as fallen trees in the inland Tallahassee area damaged around 50 vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes253_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes253-27">[27]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florida_counties_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="841" data-file-width="865" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Florida_counties_map.png/220px-Florida_counties_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Florida_counties_map.png/330px-Florida_counties_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Florida_counties_map.png/440px-Florida_counties_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florida_counties_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMap of Florida\'s counties: click to enlarge</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Though Hurricane Elena never crossed Florida\'s coast, its drawn-out interaction with land agitated large swaths of the state\'s western shore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cs_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-cs-56">[56]</a></sup> Winds along and around the Pinellas County coast generally blew from the south or southwest for several days, creating persistent onshore flow that built up heavy seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinellas_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pinellas-57">[57]</a></sup> Near Clearwater, waves reached 8.2\xc2\xa0ft (2.5\xc2\xa0m) in height, marked by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_period" title="Wave period">period</a> of 13 seconds on August 31.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge6_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge6-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge18_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge18-58">[58]</a></sup> One study determined that the storm removed an average of 10 cubic yards of <a href="/wiki/Coastal_erosion" title="Coastal erosion">coastal material</a> per linear foot of shoreline in <a href="/wiki/Escambia_County,_Florida" title="Escambia County, Florida">Escambia County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_County,_Florida" title="Gulf County, Florida">Gulf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_County,_Florida" title="Franklin County, Florida">Franklin</a>, and Pinellas counties, with values peaking at 15.6 cubic yards per foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cs_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-cs-56">[56]</a></sup> Along the predominantly <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshy</a> coasts of <a href="/wiki/Pasco_County,_Florida" title="Pasco County, Florida">Pasco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hernando_County,_Florida" title="Hernando County, Florida">Hernando</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Citrus_County,_Florida" title="Citrus County, Florida">Citrus</a> counties, erosion and structural damage were much more limited, partly due to the local southerly or southeasterly wind direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In some cases, the hurricane left quasi-permanent alterations on beaches and small islands. For example, North Bunces Key\xe2\x80\x94an island of southern Pinellas County\xe2\x80\x94lost most of its vegetation to the storm, and overwashing shifted the southern part of the island up to 330\xc2\xa0ft (100 m) from its original settlement. More extensive changes were seen on and near <a href="/wiki/Caladesi_Island_State_Park" title="Caladesi Island State Park">Caladesi Island</a>, which formed in 1921 after a <a href="/wiki/1921_Tampa_Bay_hurricane" title="1921 Tampa Bay hurricane">hurricane</a> split a larger barrier island into two by a new channel. The inlet became dominant over Dunedin Pass to the south, which grew narrower very gradually until Elena rearranged the dynamics of the beach, allowing Dunedin Pass to fill completely with sand within a couple years of the hurricane\'s passage. As a result, <a href="/wiki/Clearwater_Beach" title="Clearwater Beach">Clearwater Beach</a> became connected to Caladesi Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sediment_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-sediment-60">[60]</a></sup> Elena also created a new inlet known as Willy\'s Cut, which existed until 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-willy_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-willy-61">[61]</a></sup> Interest in artificially reopening Dunedin Pass prompted an official study in 1994 on the engineering and financial merits of such a project. Due to the high cost of dredging and the likelihood of nearly continuous maintenance, no action was taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_Florida_beach_house_damaged.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="402" data-file-width="600" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Hurricane_Elena_Florida_beach_house_damaged.jpg/300px-Hurricane_Elena_Florida_beach_house_damaged.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Hurricane_Elena_Florida_beach_house_damaged.jpg/450px-Hurricane_Elena_Florida_beach_house_damaged.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/25/Hurricane_Elena_Florida_beach_house_damaged.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_Florida_beach_house_damaged.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nExample of a beachfront structure destroyed by the hurricane in Florida</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The hurricane tore two barges from their moorings in <a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay" title="Tampa Bay">Tampa Bay</a> and blew them into <a href="/wiki/Gandy_Bridge" title="Gandy Bridge">Gandy Bridge</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes253_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes253-27">[27]</a></sup> leaving the bridge with unspecified damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tampa_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-tampa-63">[63]</a></sup> Throughout the area, rising waters inundated streets, washed boats ashore, and destroyed numerous homes along the coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tampa2_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-tampa2-64">[64]</a></sup> At low-lying Cedar Key to the north, storm surge exceeded 9\xc2\xa0ft (2.7\xc2\xa0m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes253_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes253-27">[27]</a></sup> There, and at <a href="/wiki/Alligator_Point,_Florida" title="Alligator Point, Florida">Alligator Point</a> to the northwest, the surge values represented <a href="/wiki/Return_period" title="Return period">return periods</a> of 25 to 30 years; elsewhere, they were equivalent to about 10-year events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge2_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge2-65">[65]</a></sup> Floodwaters in Cedar Key rose to 8\xc2\xa0ft (2.4\xc2\xa0m) in depth, with 2\xc2\xa0ft (0.61\xc2\xa0m) waves atop the standing water. The extent of structural damage was largely dependent on construction type, as newer, elevated buildings fared much better than older structures nearer sea level. Waterfront restaurants were especially susceptible; winds blew out several large sliding-glass doors at one establishment, allowing both the winds and the tide to enter its interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge6_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge6-49">[49]</a></sup> Elena severed the sole bridge to Cedar Key, temporarily isolating the city and stranding several residents. The <a href="/wiki/Florida_Department_of_Transportation" title="Florida Department of Transportation">Florida Department of Transportation</a> hurriedly worked to make the bridge passable long enough to rescue the stranded individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The hurricane compromised several other roads, destroying a 75\xc2\xa0ft (23\xc2\xa0m) section of <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_24" title="Florida State Road 24">State Road 24</a>. Monetary losses in Cedar Key alone were estimated at $2 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and all major aspects of local infrastructure were severely affected, initially preventing residents from returning home to the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-graham_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-graham-68">[68]</a></sup> At least 34 homes and businesses on the island were damaged or destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedarkey_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-cedarkey-69">[69]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Pinellas County suffered some of the worst damage from Hurricane Elena in Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge12_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge12-55">[55]</a></sup> At the height of the storm, over 500,000 of its residents were without electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1million_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-1million-21">[21]</a></sup> Forty-four single-family homes were destroyed, 31 more were damaged, and several condominiums, townhouses, and commercial buildings were damaged or destroyed. The hurricane also wrecked or irreparably compromised nearly 2.7\xc2\xa0mi (4.3\xc2\xa0km) of coastal bulkheads and inflicted minor damage on 2.15\xc2\xa0mi (3.46\xc2\xa0km) more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge13_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge13-70">[70]</a></sup> Most of the affected seawalls were degrading or poorly reinforced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge7_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge7-71">[71]</a></sup> Seawalls with higher standards of construction generally remained intact, though even in those cases, overwash from the Gulf of Mexico topped the barriers and deposited large volumes of sand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge8_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge8-72">[72]</a></sup> The hurricane cost roughly $100 million in Pinellas County.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redcross_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-redcross-24">[24]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_1985_Florida_boats_debris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="395" data-file-width="600" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Hurricane_Elena_1985_Florida_boats_debris.jpg/310px-Hurricane_Elena_1985_Florida_boats_debris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Hurricane_Elena_1985_Florida_boats_debris.jpg/465px-Hurricane_Elena_1985_Florida_boats_debris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/64/Hurricane_Elena_1985_Florida_boats_debris.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_1985_Florida_boats_debris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe storm washed maritime debris, such as small vessels and parts of docks, over land.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Elena\'s track parallel to the Florida Panhandle subjected the coastline between Apalachicola and Pensacola Beach to particularly severe conditions that resulted in "significant" property damage there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sr_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-sr-73">[73]</a></sup> In Apalachicola proper, winds tore large roofs off buildings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> and data from Florida\'s Department of Natural Resources indicate that 20 residences and one community building in Franklin County were damaged or destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge15_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge15-75">[75]</a></sup> Structural failure was prevalent along the county\'s waterfront and on islands such as <a href="/wiki/Dog_Island_(Florida)" title="Dog Island (Florida)">Dog Island</a>; however, it was mainly limited to poorly constructed buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge8_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge8-72">[72]</a></sup> Several miles of roadways in the county sustained significant damage, and about <span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>\xe2\x81\x84<sub>2</sub></span> mile (800\xc2\xa0m) of <a href="/wiki/Bulkhead_(barrier)" title="Bulkhead (barrier)">bulkhead</a> was destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge15_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge15-75">[75]</a></sup> Low seawalls allowed crucial points of the causeway to <a href="/wiki/St._George_Island_(Florida)" title="St. George Island (Florida)">St. George Island</a> to erode, causing it to fail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodge8_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bodge8-72">[72]</a></sup> In Escambia County, the hurricane left $2 million in damages. Throughout much of the remainder of the Florida Panhandle, structural damage was limited, though 100,000 people in the Pensacola area lost power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The configuration of the southern tip of <a href="/wiki/Cape_San_Blas" title="Cape San Blas">Cape San Blas</a> in Gulf County was changed by the storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The hurricane wiped out eagle nests, generated freshwater fish kills, and affected other species of wildlife at the <a href="/wiki/St._Vincent_National_Wildlife_Refuge" title="St. Vincent National Wildlife Refuge">St. Vincent</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Marks_National_Wildlife_Refuge" title="St. Marks National Wildlife Refuge">St. Marks</a> National Wildlife Refuges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cs_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-cs-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/St._George_Island_State_Park" title="St. George Island State Park">St. George Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/Honeymoon_Island_State_Park" title="Honeymoon Island State Park">Honeymoon Island</a> State Parks were heavily affected, with appreciable but lesser damage in numerous other protected areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmsd21_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-mmsd21-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_98_in_Florida" title="U.S. Route 98 in Florida">U.S. Route 98</a>, which closely follows the coast in this region, required extensive repairs after being undermined in nearly two dozen locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>\n<p>When tropical cyclones move over land, they often produce the wind shear and <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_instability" title="Atmospheric instability">atmospheric instability</a> required for the development of weak, embedded supercell thunderstorms, which can produce tornadoes. These tornadoes are usually weak and short-lived, but still capable of producing significant damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> While centered over the Gulf of Mexico, the eastern side of Hurricane Elena\'s circulation spawned several such tornadoes over central Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks17_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks17-9">[9]</a></sup> A tornado struck just east of <a href="/wiki/Leesburg,_Florida" title="Leesburg, Florida">Leesburg</a> on September 1, destroying 64 single-family houses and mobile homes, and damaging another 118; seven people were treated for non-life-threatening injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Later that same day, another tornado touched down over downtown Leesburg, with much less damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marion_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-marion-82">[82]</a></sup> In nearby <a href="/wiki/Marion_County,_Florida" title="Marion County, Florida">Marion County</a>, tornadic activity destroyed six mobile homes, compromised another 50 residences, and inflicted as much as $500,000 in total losses, though only minor injuries were reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center_Launch_Complex_39" title="Kennedy Space Center Launch Complex 39">Kennedy Space Center Launch Complex 39</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral,_Florida" title="Cape Canaveral, Florida">Cape Canaveral</a>, a weak tornado struck two vehicles near the location where <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis">Space Shuttle Atlantis</a> was being prepared for its first flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes254_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes254-84">[84]</a></sup> A tornado in <a href="/wiki/New_Port_Richey,_Florida" title="New Port Richey, Florida">New Port Richey</a> tore parts of the roof off at least one building and brought down trees,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-graham_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-graham-68">[68]</a></sup> and tornadic activity was also identified in <a href="/wiki/Sumter_County,_Florida" title="Sumter County, Florida">Sumter County</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes254_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes254-84">[84]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The hurricane took one life in the state and indirectly contributed to two additional deaths. In <a href="/wiki/Daytona_Beach,_Florida" title="Daytona Beach, Florida">Daytona Beach</a>, a tree struck a parked vehicle, killing a person inside. The exact cause of the tree\'s uprooting was unknown, although it may have been hit by lightning or a short-lived tornado.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Elsewhere, two individuals died of heart attacks: one while installing storm shutters on his home, and another at a designated shelter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roars_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-roars-32">[32]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alabama">Alabama</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Alabama">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_NOAA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="800" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Hurricane_Elena_NOAA.jpg/220px-Hurricane_Elena_NOAA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Hurricane_Elena_NOAA.jpg/330px-Hurricane_Elena_NOAA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Hurricane_Elena_NOAA.jpg/440px-Hurricane_Elena_NOAA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_NOAA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nSatellite view of the hurricane\'s core on September 1, while traveling westward</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The center of Elena passed 30\xc2\xa0mi (50\xc2\xa0km) south of mainland Alabama as it accelerated toward the Gulf Coast, affecting the state\'s two-county coast and offshore islands. Wind gusts at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dauphin_Island" title="Dauphin Island">Dauphin Island</a>, situated much closer to the hurricane\'s eye, were estimated to have reached 130\xc2\xa0mph (210\xc2\xa0km/h); these velocities represented some of the highest experienced on land from the storm,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup> and were strong enough to snap hundreds of large pine trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bama2_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bama2-86">[86]</a></sup> Dauphin Island received an 8.4\xc2\xa0ft (2.6\xc2\xa0m) storm surge that resulted in substantial flooding and areas of total overwash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bama2_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bama2-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata28_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata28-87">[87]</a></sup> Rainfall amounted to just 3\xc2\xa0in (76\xc2\xa0mm) on the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup></p>\n<p>With its location close to the storm\'s center, Dauphin Island saw the greatest damage in Alabama. Access to the island was shut down during and immediately after the hurricane, slowing the progression of damage assessments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alabama_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-alabama-88">[88]</a></sup> Additionally, the storm cut power and phone services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Star-Banner_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Star-Banner-89">[89]</a></sup> Post-storm surveys revealed discernible patterns in structural damage on the island; these included a nearly complete lack of destruction on the heavily wooded eastern end, and damage concentrated closer to the western side and along areas exposed to strong easterly winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks80_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks80-90">[90]</a></sup> First-hand accounts relayed that in the most severe cases, entire elevated homes were torn from their <a href="/wiki/Deep_foundation" title="Deep foundation">pilings</a> and swept into the Gulf of Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The number of homes demolished in that manner was informally placed at 50,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dauphin_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-dauphin-92">[92]</a></sup> though such total building collapses were typically confined to poorly secured buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks80_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks80-90">[90]</a></sup> In total, the hurricane destroyed 190 residences on Dauphin Island, accounting for nearly 25% of all homes, and a further 235 sustained substantial damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dauphin_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-dauphin-92">[92]</a></sup> An unofficial and early estimate of losses on the island was $30 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bama2_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bama2-86">[86]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Windspeeds were markedly lower over mainland Alabama;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormtrack_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-stormtrack-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mobile,_Alabama" title="Mobile, Alabama">Mobile</a> recorded winds of over 50\xc2\xa0mph (80\xc2\xa0km/h),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup> with gusts as high as 84\xc2\xa0mph (135\xc2\xa0km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata28_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata28-87">[87]</a></sup> The storm\'s angle of approach created strong offshore winds along the mainland, which depressed water levels and limited the extent of positive surge once winds shifted to onshore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s23_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-s23-94">[94]</a></sup> Winds from the hurricane took a toll on crops, ruining 8,000,000\xc2\xa0lb (3,600,000\xc2\xa0kg) of pecans and reducing soybean production by 10%. Farms were still in the process of recovering from <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Frederic" title="Hurricane Frederic">Hurricane Frederic</a> in 1979 when Elena struck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata28_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata28-87">[87]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Wave action took a toll on the foundations of waterfront structures along the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_County,_Alabama" title="Baldwin County, Alabama">Baldwin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mobile_County,_Alabama" title="Mobile County, Alabama">Mobile</a> counties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormtrack_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-stormtrack-93">[93]</a></sup> where Elena inflicted about $715,000 worth of damage to roadways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mineralsd19_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-mineralsd19-95">[95]</a></sup> Most damage was concentrated near the shore, where extensive erosion took place, and on islands and minor peninsulas. Farther inland, Elena\'s impact was generally limited to downed trees and power lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata28_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata28-87">[87]</a></sup> The storm destroyed the city boardwalk at <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Shores,_Alabama" title="Gulf Shores, Alabama">Gulf Shores</a>, with the cost of rebuilding expected to approach $300,000. <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Power" title="Alabama Power">Alabama Power</a> reported extensive power outages affecting up to 100,000 customers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bama2_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bama2-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alabama_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-alabama-88">[88]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Insurance_Information_Institute" title="Insurance Information Institute">Insurance Information Institute</a>, storm-related damages in Alabama totaled about $100 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> An estimated 300 homes in the state were destroyed by Hurricane Elena, and another 1,345 sustained lighter damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata28_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata28-87">[87]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mississippi">Mississippi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mississippi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Along the coast of Mississippi, where Elena made landfall, the most significant effects of the storm stemmed from its strong winds gusting to over 120\xc2\xa0mph (190\xc2\xa0km/h). Recorded gusts included 121\xc2\xa0mph (195\xc2\xa0km/h) at <a href="/wiki/Gulfport,_Mississippi" title="Gulfport, Mississippi">Gulfport</a>, 115\xc2\xa0mph (185\xc2\xa0km/h) at Pascagoula, and 90\xc2\xa0mph (140\xc2\xa0km/h) at Biloxi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim2_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-prelim2-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks15_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks15-97">[97]</a></sup> Several other weather stations clocked sustained winds at over 90\xc2\xa0mph (140\xc2\xa0km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup> Consistent with the storm\'s dry nature, rainfall in the state was mainly light and confined to southern and western areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpc_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-hpc-5">[5]</a></sup> Gulfport picked up more than 4.5\xc2\xa0in (110\xc2\xa0mm),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulfport_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-gulfport-98">[98]</a></sup> while just over 3 in (75\xc2\xa0mm) of rain fell at <a href="/wiki/Natchez,_Mississippi" title="Natchez, Mississippi">Natchez</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup> Some streets in Gulfport and Biloxi flooded at the height of the storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulfport_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-gulfport-98">[98]</a></sup> The highest tides ran 6 to 8\xc2\xa0ft (1.8 to 2.4\xc2\xa0m) above normal along the coast,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks16_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks16-51">[51]</a></sup> reaching 7.9\xc2\xa0ft (2.4\xc2\xa0m) above average at Pascagoula and <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Springs,_Mississippi" title="Ocean Springs, Mississippi">Ocean Springs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-9"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup> As in Alabama, negative surge values were recorded at the storm\'s onset. The tide gauge at Gulfport recorded a water level of 5.6\xc2\xa0ft (1.7\xc2\xa0m) below average early on September 2, before quickly swelling to 5.43\xc2\xa0ft (1.66\xc2\xa0m) above normal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s23_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-s23-94">[94]</a></sup> Air pressure reportedly fell so rapidly at Pascagoula that car windows began to shatter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes254_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes254-84">[84]</a></sup> The barometer there bottomed out at 953 mb, the lowest pressure recorded on land in association with the cyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks17_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks17-9">[9]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elena_1985_rainfall.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="892" data-file-width="1002" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Elena_1985_rainfall.gif/220px-Elena_1985_rainfall.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Elena_1985_rainfall.gif/330px-Elena_1985_rainfall.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Elena_1985_rainfall.gif/440px-Elena_1985_rainfall.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elena_1985_rainfall.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nElena\'s storm total rainfall in the United States</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The worst of the damage occurred along a 40\xc2\xa0mi (64\xc2\xa0km) stretch of coastline, centered on the Pascagoula area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulfport_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-gulfport-98">[98]</a></sup> Elena\'s winds damaged most of the schools in <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Mississippi" title="Jackson County, Mississippi">Jackson County</a>, and more specifically, every school in Pascagoula was structurally compromised to some degree. Damages to schools in Ocean Springs totaled $3 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks50_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks50-99">[99]</a></sup> In the same city, the overall conditions following the hurricane were described as worse than those in the prior hurricanes Frederic or <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Camille" title="Hurricane Camille">Camille</a>. Elena destroyed 20 houses and two supermarkets in Ocean Springs, and several buildings on every city block sustained severe roof damage due to fallen trees. Two shopping centers were destroyed in nearby <a href="/wiki/Gautier,_Mississippi" title="Gautier, Mississippi">Gautier</a>, possibly by short-lived tornadoes. A <a href="/wiki/Fire_captain" title="Fire captain">fire captain</a> in Gautier remarked immediately after the storm that he had not yet seen an unharmed building in the city. The community became essentially isolated from the outside world, and quickly began to run short of food, clean water, and gasoline supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-100">[100]</a></sup> Initial reports from Pascagoula also indicated that most, or all, of that city\'s buildings were damaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bangor_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-bangor-101">[101]</a></sup> Nearly every business in the city was damaged to the extent that none were able to open when the storm cleared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harlan_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-harlan-35">[35]</a></sup> A post-storm assessment by the Mississippi Emergency Management Agency revealed over 900 businesses in Jackson County sustained damage, contributing to a total of nearly 1,500 in the state\'s three coastal counties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miaftermath_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-miaftermath-102">[102]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Harrison_County,_Mississippi" title="Harrison County, Mississippi">Harrison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hancock_County,_Mississippi" title="Hancock County, Mississippi">Hancock</a> counties were generally not affected quite as severely as communities closer to the Alabama border, but the entire area still suffered extensively. In sections of Gulfport, large fires were sparked by downed power lines and fed by broken natural gas pipes. Debris on roadways prevented firefighters from reaching the fires, allowing them to spread. Similar destruction was seen to the east at Biloxi, where the hurricane\'s winds tore the roofs off many buildings. Beachfront communities were in a state of disarray, with large trees uprooted, debris littering the ground, and accumulations of sand on parts of roads like U.S. 90.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulfport_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-gulfport-98">[98]</a></sup> Damage to schools in Harrison County\xe2\x80\x94particularly in Gulfport and Biloxi\xe2\x80\x94was extensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Most homes in the area survived the storm, which was locally estimated to have been a once-in-50-year event, with relatively little damage. Several buildings along the coast in the Biloxi area sustained severe damage, but many of the older houses near the Gulf of Mexico there fared remarkably well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulfport_98-4"><a href="#cite_note-gulfport-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Winds brought down large highway signs, in some cases striking nearby buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Along the coast, Elena caused beach erosion, damaged coastal structures and recreational beach facilities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and dislocated navigational buoys and markers in various ports, several of which were closed pending Coast Guard inspection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usa_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-usa-107">[107]</a></sup> Winds over inland <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_County,_Mississippi" title="Pearl River County, Mississippi">Pearl River County</a> damaged 350 permanent and mobile homes, and as in Alabama, the hurricane took a large toll on pecan and soybean crops and farms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata36_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata36-108">[108]</a></sup></p>\n<p>There were numerous reports in southern Mississippi of embedded tornadoes that exacerbated the hurricane\'s effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim3_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-prelim3-36">[36]</a></sup> Reports in Gulfport indicated that three schools actively being used as hurricane shelters were struck and damaged by tornadoes. At one location, almost 400 people being housed in a school had to rush to safety before part of its roof collapsed. Another apparent tornado sideswiped a senior citizens\' center, endangering nearly 200 people in a structure that sustained damage to windows, doors, and part of its roof; about 20 people required rescue by paramedics. Only minor injuries occurred in association with the possible tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulfport_98-5"><a href="#cite_note-gulfport-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biloxi_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-biloxi-109">[109]</a></sup> Teams of experts tasked with reviewing the validity of tornado reports were largely unable to prove that a significant portion of the damage in southern Mississippi had been done by tornadoes.
