As a result, few tornadoes were confirmed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparks17_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sparks17-9">[9]</a></sup> and it is likely that most of the damage in the region resulted from squall-like winds that are part of an intense hurricane\'s nature, or potentially localized <a href="/wiki/Microburst" title="Microburst">microbursts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prelim3_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-prelim3-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata36_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata36-108">[108]</a></sup> Any unconfirmed tornadoes would have been no stronger than the hurricane\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synoptic_scale" title="Synoptic scale">synoptic</a> winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata36_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata36-108">[108]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elena_02_sep_1985_1411Z_N8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="1300" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Elena_02_sep_1985_1411Z_N8.jpg/220px-Elena_02_sep_1985_1411Z_N8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Elena_02_sep_1985_1411Z_N8.jpg/330px-Elena_02_sep_1985_1411Z_N8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Elena_02_sep_1985_1411Z_N8.jpg/440px-Elena_02_sep_1985_1411Z_N8.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elena_02_sep_1985_1411Z_N8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nSatellite image of Elena making landfall on September\xc2\xa02</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The storm left 80,000 Mississippi Power Company customers without electricity; most of Jackson County\'s 126,000 residents were affected by the outage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miaftermath_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-miaftermath-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ingalls_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-ingalls-110">[110]</a></sup> Operations at <a href="/wiki/Ingalls_Shipbuilding" title="Ingalls Shipbuilding">Ingalls Shipbuilding</a> in Pascagoula were halted due to the power outage and widespread damage to the shipyard\'s buildings and cranes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ingalls_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-ingalls-110">[110]</a></sup> and at least two other shipyards in the state were affected by the hurricane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmsd12_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-mmsd12-111">[111]</a></sup> Additionally, the storm forced the temporary closure of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevron_USA" title="Chevron USA">Chevron USA</a> refinery at Pascagoula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usa_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-usa-107">[107]</a></sup> Facilities at <a href="/wiki/Horn_Island_(Mississippi)" title="Horn Island (Mississippi)">Horn Island</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Islands_National_Seashore" title="Gulf Islands National Seashore">Gulf Islands National Seashore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buccaneer_State_Park" title="Buccaneer State Park">Buccaneer State Park</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Sandhill_Crane_National_Wildlife_Refuge" title="Mississippi Sandhill Crane National Wildlife Refuge">Mississippi Sandhill Crane National Wildlife Refuge</a> required repairs following the storm; at the latter, damages included the cost of healing a <a href="/wiki/Sandhill_crane" title="Sandhill crane">Florida sandhill crane</a>\'s injured leg. Thirty seafood processing plants were damaged, and one was destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmsd12_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-mmsd12-111">[111]</a></sup> The Red Cross estimated that 200 single-family houses in the state were destroyed, and some 13,200 were damaged, 1,200 of them heavily. Additionally, the hurricane demolished 390 mobile homes and damaged another 2,290.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bogalusa_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-bogalusa-112">[112]</a></sup> The overall cost of damage in Mississippi alone approached $1 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata36_108-3"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata36-108">[108]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Louisiana">Louisiana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Louisiana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After moving inland, the storm\'s northwestward track brought it over the Louisiana border on two separate occasions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpc_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-hpc-5">[5]</a></sup> first reaching <a href="/wiki/Washington_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Washington Parish, Louisiana">Washington Parish</a> as a minimal hurricane. Winds there were strong enough to bring down hundreds of trees, damaging houses and knocking out power to over 15,000 customers in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata33_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata33-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-louisiana_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-louisiana-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The hurricane also overturned mobile homes and strewn debris throughout communities such as <a href="/wiki/Bogalusa,_Louisiana" title="Bogalusa, Louisiana">Bogalusa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franklinton,_Louisiana" title="Franklinton, Louisiana">Franklinton</a> in Washington Parish, the hardest-hit area in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-la_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-la-31">[31]</a></sup> Downed trees caused damage to 200 homes and another 200 businesses, chiefly near Bogalusa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata33_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata33-113">[113]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Winds across the rest of the state were moderate, gusting to around 50\xc2\xa0mph (80\xc2\xa0km/h) at <a href="/wiki/Slidell,_Louisiana" title="Slidell, Louisiana">Slidell</a> on the northeastern shore of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain" title="Lake Pontchartrain">Lake Pontchartrain</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-10"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup> so damage outside of Washington Parish was sporadic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata33_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata33-113">[113]</a></sup> Throughout the state, at least 40,000 electric customers lost power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bangor_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-bangor-101">[101]</a></sup> Located southwest of the storm\'s core, New Orleans escaped with little damage and relatively benign weather conditions; still, the hurricane triggered minor flooding and brought down tree limbs around the city. Levees along the shores of Lake Pontchartrain were able to contain the lake\'s large waves, despite initial fears to the contrary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pont_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-pont-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-la_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-la-31">[31]</a></sup> Still, the adverse conditions forced the temporary closure of the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain_Causeway" title="Lake Pontchartrain Causeway">Lake Pontchartrain Causeway</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The storm led to the deaths of two individuals in the state: one due to a drowning in <a href="/wiki/St._Tammany_Parish,_Louisiana" title="St. Tammany Parish, Louisiana">St. Tammany Parish</a> and another in a traffic accident attributed to the weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-la_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-la-31">[31]</a></sup> Insured and uninsured damages were worth near $17 million combined, with an additional $500,000 in agricultural losses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata33_113-3"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata33-113">[113]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Elena subjected the <a href="/wiki/Chandeleur_Islands" title="Chandeleur Islands">Chandeleur Islands</a> to a 6.5\xc2\xa0ft (2.0\xc2\xa0m) or greater storm surge. The island chain is an important buffer to parts of mainland Louisiana against storms, but is frequently reshaped or shrunken by intense hurricanes. <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Danny_(1985)" title="Hurricane Danny (1985)">Hurricane Danny</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Juan_(1985)" title="Hurricane Juan (1985)">Hurricane Juan</a> also affected the islands in 1985. Elena eroded away at least 20% and possibly up to 40% of the Chandeleur Islands\' total land mass and cut 30 significant channels into the island chain. Parts of the islands left intact suffered extensive loss of vegetation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata33_113-4"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata33-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmsd7_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-mmsd7-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The hurricane, along with Danny and Juan, also affected several other barrier islands, and Elena itself removed as much as 112\xc2\xa0ft (34\xc2\xa0m) of beach along the island of <a href="/wiki/Grand_Isle,_Louisiana" title="Grand Isle, Louisiana">Grand Isle, Louisiana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmsd8_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-mmsd8-119">[119]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elsewhere">Elsewhere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Elsewhere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Precipitation from Hurricane Elena reached into southern <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> and parts of <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpc_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-hpc-5">[5]</a></sup> with little impact aside from meager drought relief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> For several days after landfall, the weakening tropical cyclone produced moderate to heavy rainfall across portions of central and northern Arkansas. Rainfall totals were generally 2 to 4\xc2\xa0in (51 to 102\xc2\xa0mm), with locally higher amounts; <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Home,_Arkansas" title="Mountain Home, Arkansas">Mountain Home, Arkansas</a> received 8.95\xc2\xa0in (227\xc2\xa0mm) of rain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-11"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arkansas_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-arkansas-121">[121]</a></sup> including 6.6\xc2\xa0in (170\xc2\xa0mm) in just three hours on September 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mountain_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-mountain-122">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Clinton,_Arkansas" title="Clinton, Arkansas">Clinton</a> to the south recorded 8.6\xc2\xa0in (220\xc2\xa0mm). At the state capital of <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock,_Arkansas" title="Little Rock, Arkansas">Little Rock</a>, under 3\xc2\xa0in (76\xc2\xa0mm) of liquid fell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwr_11-12"><a href="#cite_note-mwr-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arkansas_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-arkansas-121">[121]</a></sup> Listed by the <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> among "some of the most significant tropical cyclones to affect Arkansas", the remnants of Elena triggered flash flooding in parts of four counties; 2\xc2\xa0ft (0.61\xc2\xa0m) of standing water submerged streets in downtown <a href="/wiki/Hot_Springs,_Arkansas" title="Hot Springs, Arkansas">Hot Springs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arkansas_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-arkansas-121">[121]</a></sup> In Mountain Home, floodwaters forced 10 families to evacuate their homes, and one person died after a swollen creek swept her car off a bridge spanning it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mountain_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-mountain-122">[122]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Significant rainfall also occurred over parts of western Kentucky, with lighter precipitation in several adjacent states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hpc_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-hpc-5">[5]</a></sup> Over 8\xc2\xa0in (200\xc2\xa0mm) fell at <a href="/wiki/Paducah,_Kentucky" title="Paducah, Kentucky">Paducah</a>, where urban streets and low-lying terrain experienced freshwater flooding Floodwaters 4\xc2\xa0ft (1.2\xc2\xa0m) deep submerged cars to their windows and infiltrated 40 homes, several businesses, a hotel, and a high school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stormdata32-33_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-stormdata32-33-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harlan2_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-harlan2-124">[124]</a></sup> Localized evacuations and road closures were required, and a person wading in an active creek had to be rescued after the creek swept him downstream. Around 10,000 customers lost electric service for a short period due to the storm. Shelters were opened to those displaced by the flooding, but scarcely used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harlan2_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-harlan2-124">[124]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Early in its formative stages, Elena triggered rainshowers and thunderstorms over parts of Cuba, <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">The Bahamas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Later, the mature hurricane generated strong <a href="/wiki/Rip_current" title="Rip current">rip currents</a> as far away as <a href="/wiki/South_Padre_Island,_Texas" title="South Padre Island, Texas">South Padre Island, Texas</a>, where two swimmers drowned in separate incidents over the Labor Day weekend. Both victims were male Texas residents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Hurricane Elena has a multifaceted legacy; it is remembered not only for its severe impacts, but also for its unpredictability and the wide extent of pre-storm preparations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes255_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes255-39">[39]</a></sup> Due to its notoriety, the name <i>Elena</i> was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricanes" title="List of retired Atlantic hurricanes">retired</a> from the cyclical list of Atlantic hurricane names in the spring of 1986. Consequently, it will never again be used for an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_tropical_cyclone" title="North Atlantic tropical cyclone">Atlantic hurricane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> The name was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Erika" title="Tropical Storm Erika"><i>Erika</i></a>, which was first used during the <a href="/wiki/1991_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1991 Atlantic hurricane season">1991 season</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hurdat_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-hurdat-3">[3]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_Florida_house_damage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="397" data-file-width="600" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Hurricane_Elena_Florida_house_damage.jpg/220px-Hurricane_Elena_Florida_house_damage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Hurricane_Elena_Florida_house_damage.jpg/330px-Hurricane_Elena_Florida_house_damage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Hurricane_Elena_Florida_house_damage.jpg/440px-Hurricane_Elena_Florida_house_damage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Elena_Florida_house_damage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThousands of families required temporary housing after their homes were rendered unlivable.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Florida_2">Florida</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Florida">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The state of Florida received a federal Major Disaster Declaration on September 12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Franklin, Levy, Manatee, and Pinellas counties\xe2\x80\x94where the storm left 5,000 individuals without work\xe2\x80\x94became eligible for federal aid after President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> visited the state and determined that residents in those areas would benefit from assistance such as temporary housing, low-interest loans for rebuilding efforts, and monetary grants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedarkey_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-cedarkey-69">[69]</a></sup> Disaster centers were opened in those four counties as centralized locations for federal, state, and volunteer agencies to operate relief programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> President Reagan later included Hillsborough, Wakulla, and Dixie counties, bringing the total number of Florida counties eligible for federal aid to seven. The deadline for residents of all seven counties to apply for either state or federal assistance was set for November 12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Several major corporations\xe2\x80\x94including <a href="/wiki/Texaco" title="Texaco">Texaco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exxon" title="Exxon">Exxon</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.C._Penney" title="J.C. Penney">J.C. Penney</a>\xe2\x80\x94contacted customers in the affected areas and offered to make special arrangements for their monthly payments if they had been financially affected by the storm. While only a small number of customers took advantage of the assistance, the companies\' actions were met with highly positive feedback.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In the days following the storm, residents of Cedar Key were forbidden from returning to their homes and businesses while washed-out roadways underwent repairs and debris was cleared. Portable toilets were delivered and clean water trucked in for use while the city\'s infrastructure was being stabilized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> After the city of Cedar Key dropped its participation in the <a href="/wiki/National_Flood_Insurance_Program" title="National Flood Insurance Program">National Flood Insurance Program</a> in early 1984, leaving residents unable to purchase flood insurance for their property, the city council voted unanimously to return to the program after Hurricane Elena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedarkey_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-cedarkey-69">[69]</a></sup> Tourism decreased significantly in some areas due to prospective travelers\' concerns about the extent of the damage. The hurricane created a 13% drop in visitors between October 1984 and October 1985 in Pinellas County, marking an early end to the annual "tourist season", which generally ends after Labor Day weekend; tourist spending fell accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>\n<p>After the storm, residents were allowed to return to their neighborhoods on a by-town basis. Once authorized to enter their communities, many individuals inadvertently gained access to other municipalities in the area that were not ready for the return of civilians. In turn, dangerous situations arose amid preliminary cleanup operations. In Pinellas County, laws were proposed to unify the municipal decisions to accept residents after future disasters. As part of the proposed laws, the county sheriff, as opposed to local officials, would become responsible for allowing cities to reopen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emergency_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-emergency-134">[134]</a></sup> Despite extensive resistance, county commissioners approved the change, giving the sitting sheriff power to override municipal evacuation orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-approve_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-approve-135">[135]</a></sup> An additional ordinance was proposed to allow banning of alcohol sales during emergencies. During Hurricane Elena, intoxicated individuals created disorder at shelters and impeded evacuations by refusing to leave <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_party" title="Hurricane party">hurricane parties</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emergency_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-emergency-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-approve_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-approve-135">[135]</a></sup></p>\n<p>To help the Apalachicola Bay shellfish industry recover, special regulations were put in place to monitor harvests, and $2 million was designated toward rehabilitation efforts. Using a portion of the funds, out-of-work oyster catchers were employed to repopulate crucial reefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apa_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-apa-44">[44]</a></sup> The state of Florida also issued a grant to help individuals in the crippled seafood industry make necessary payments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Efforts to help newly unemployed individuals in the shellfish industry continued in the months following the storm; local Tallahassee musicians organized a benefit concert in January 1986 to raise money for families of oystermen in Franklin County.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>\n<p>On September 1 and 2, Florida Power Company received help from Gulf Coast companies to return power to 170,000 customers before the assisting companies\' home areas were struck by the hurricane. Power was restored to most areas by September 4, with an exception being St. George\'s Island; service was expected to be restored after several additional days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-550re_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-550re-37">[37]</a></sup> More broadly, owners of heavily damaged homes in the state faced new regulations on coastal construction in the state, which went into effect less than a month after the storm. The new rules entailed more rigorous study of factors such as a property\'s prior history and surrounding buildings before approval to rebuild a demolished structure would be granted. Governor Graham preliminarily advised that houses more than halfway destroyed not be rebuilt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Recovery efforts after Hurricane Elena continued to a small degree for years after its passage; for example, <a href="/wiki/Beach_nourishment" title="Beach nourishment">beach replenishment</a> at <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rocks_Beach,_Florida" title="Indian Rocks Beach, Florida">Indian Rocks Beach</a> in Pinellas County began in the summer of 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Gulf_Coast">Central Gulf Coast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Central Gulf Coast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Power companies from several states sent workers to help restore service to the hardest-hit areas of the Gulf Coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-after_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-after-40">[40]</a></sup> Most of Alabama Power\'s affected customers had power within 24 hours of the storm, though restoration of service to Dauphin Island took significantly longer. Power was fully restored to <a href="/wiki/CLECO" title="CLECO">Central Louisiana Electric</a> customers by September 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-550re_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-550re-37">[37]</a></sup> Alabama\'s two coastal counties were declared federal disaster areas on September 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ala_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-ala-141">[141]</a></sup> Special loan assistance was made available by the <a href="/wiki/Small_Business_Administration" title="Small Business Administration">Small Business Administration</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Farmers_Home_Administration" title="Farmers Home Administration">Farmers Home Administration</a>, the latter of which sought to help commercial growers who lost their crops to the storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ala_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-ala-141">[141]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Mississippi Governor <a href="/wiki/William_Allain" title="William Allain">William Allain</a> sent 500 members of the National Guard to partner with 200 law enforcement officers along Mississippi\'s coast in minimizing crime, and nighttime curfews were established in several cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-after_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-after-40">[40]</a></sup> On September 4, President Reagan declared Mississippi\'s coastal counties a Major Disaster area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miss_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-miss-143">[143]</a></sup> The Federal Emergency Management Agency estimated that as many as 3,000 homes in the state were uninhabitable, their occupants forced to find temporary living arrangements. The Small Business Administration approved special loans up to $500,000 for owners of damaged businesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miss_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-miss-143">[143]</a></sup> Mississippi Power Company\'s system was the hardest-hit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-550re_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-550re-37">[37]</a></sup> and restoration of service was slow; 50,000 of 80,000 customers were still without electricity by September 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miaftermath_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-miaftermath-102">[102]</a></sup></p>\n<p>By September 5, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salvation_Army" title="Salvation Army">Salvation Army</a>, Red Cross, and other organizations had served 100,000 meals to those displaced by the hurricane in Mississippi, and federal food stocks became available for the state to distribute to storm victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-governor_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-governor-144">[144]</a></sup> Still, resources such as food and ice started to run short in the hardest-hit locations, and long lines formed at the first few stores and gas stations to reopen. With dwindling supplies, the Salvation Army had to procure food from other parts of the region to serve to victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miaftermath_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-miaftermath-102">[102]</a></sup> In the days after the hurricane, an increase in heart attack deaths in the Harrison County area was noted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-governor_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-governor-144">[144]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Elena&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="2004" data-file-width="3032" height="21" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Cyclone_Catarina_from_the_ISS_on_March_26_2004.JPG/32px-Cyclone_Catarina_from_the_ISS_on_March_26_2004.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Cyclone_Catarina_from_the_ISS_on_March_26_2004.JPG/48px-Cyclone_Catarina_from_the_ISS_on_March_26_2004.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Cyclone_Catarina_from_the_ISS_on_March_26_2004.JPG/64px-Cyclone_Catarina_from_the_ISS_on_March_26_2004.JPG 2x" width="32"/></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Tropical_cyclones" title="Portal:Tropical cyclones">Tropical cyclones portal</a></span></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_hurricanes" title="List of United States hurricanes">List of United States hurricanes</a></li>\n</ul>\n<dl>\n<dd>\n<ul>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Florida_hurricanes_(1975%E2%80%931999)" title="List of Florida hurricanes (1975\xe2\x80\x931999)">List of Florida hurricanes (1975\xe2\x80\x931999)</a></li>\n</ul>\n</dd>\n</dl>\n<ul>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names" title="List of retired Atlantic hurricane names">List of retired Atlantic hurricane names</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_tropical_cyclone-spawned_tornadoes" title="History of tropical cyclone-spawned tornadoes">History of tropical cyclone-spawned tornadoes</a></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
